        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138"><meta property="og:title" content="Encapsulation of Metallic Na in an Electrically Conductive Host with Porous Channels as a Highly Stable Na Metal Anode"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/acs.nanolett.7b01138.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Room-temperature Na ion batteries (NIBs) have attracted great attention because of the widely available, abundant sodium resources and potentially low cost. Currently, the challenge of the NIB development is due primarily to the lack of a high-performance anode, while the Na metal anode holds great promise considering its highest specific capacity of 1165 mA h/g and lowest anodic potential. However, an uneven deposit, relatively infinite volume change, and dendritic growth upon plating/stripping cycles cause a low Coulombic efficiency, poor cycling performance, and severe safety concerns. Here, a stable Na carbonized wood (Na–wood) composite anode was fabricated via a rapid melt infusion (about 5 s) into channels of carbonized wood by capillary action. The channels function as a high-surface-area, conductive, mechanically stable skeleton, which lowers the effective current density, ensures a uniform Na nucleation, and restricts the volume change over cycles. As a result, the Na–wood composite anode exhibited flat plating/stripping profiles with smaller overpotentials and stable cycling performance over 500 h at 1.0 mA/cm2 in a common carbonate electrolyte system. In sharp comparison, the planar Na metal electrode showed a much shorter cycle life of 100 h under the same test conditions."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/acs.nanolett.7b01138.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da865f0e0cb325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da865f0e0cb325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'f130fafb-3f2d-4b20-8b05-10ed8399833d'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90da865f0e0cb325-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da865f0e0cb325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

title="Download Hi-Res Image" target="_blank" class="figure-viewer_hi-res-link"><i class="icon-file-image-o"></i><span>Download Hi-Res Image</span></a><span class="fv-separator"></span><a href="#" href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="90da865f0e0cb325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Encapsulation of Metallic Na in an Electrically Conductive Host with Porous Channels as a Highly Stable Na Metal Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Encapsulation of Metallic Na in an Electrically Conductive Host with Porous Channels as a Highly Stable Na Metal Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Encapsulation of Metallic Na in an Electrically Conductive Host with Porous Channels as a Highly Stable Na Metal Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Encapsulation of Metallic Na in an Electrically Conductive Host with Porous Channels as a Highly Stable Na Metal Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Encapsulation of Metallic Na in an Electrically Conductive Host with Porous Channels as a Highly Stable Na Metal Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Encapsulation of Metallic Na in an Electrically Conductive Host with Porous Channels as a Highly Stable Na Metal Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Encapsulation of Metallic Na in an Electrically Conductive Host with Porous Channels as a Highly Stable Na Metal Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Encapsulation of Metallic Na in an Electrically Conductive Host with Porous Channels as a Highly Stable Na Metal Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Encapsulation of Metallic Na in an Electrically Conductive Host with Porous Channels as a Highly Stable Na Metal Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="hidden" property="articleSection">rapid-communication</div><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D3792%26pageCount%3D6%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWei%2BLuo%252C%2BYing%2BZhang%252C%2BShaomao%2BXu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D17%26issueNum%3D6%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.7b01138%26title%3DEncapsulation%2Bof%2BMetallic%2BNa%2Bin%2Ban%2BElectrically%2BConductive%2BHost%2Bwith%2BPorous%2BChannels%2Bas%2Ba%2BHighly%2BStable%2BNa%2BMetal%2BAnode%26numPages%3D6%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D3797%26publicationDate%3DJune%2B2017" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01138y_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Room-temperature Na ion batteries (NIBs) have attracted great attention because of the widely available, abundant sodium resources and potentially low cost. Currently, the challenge of the NIB development is due primarily to the lack of a high-performance anode, while the Na metal anode holds great promise considering its highest specific capacity of 1165 mA h/g and lowest anodic potential. However, an uneven deposit, relatively infinite volume change, and dendritic growth upon plating/stripping cycles cause a low Coulombic efficiency, poor cycling performance, and severe safety concerns. Here, a stable Na carbonized wood (Na–wood) composite anode was fabricated via a rapid melt infusion (about 5 s) into channels of carbonized wood by capillary action. The channels function as a high-surface-area, conductive, mechanically stable skeleton, which lowers the effective current density, ensures a uniform Na nucleation, and restricts the volume change over cycles. As a result, the Na–wood composite anode exhibited flat plating/stripping profiles with smaller overpotentials and stable cycling performance over 500 h at 1.0 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> in a common carbonate electrolyte system. In sharp comparison, the planar Na metal electrode showed a much shorter cycle life of 100 h under the same test conditions.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D3792%26pageCount%3D6%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWei%2BLuo%252C%2BYing%2BZhang%252C%2BShaomao%2BXu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D17%26issueNum%3D6%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.7b01138%26title%3DEncapsulation%2Bof%2BMetallic%2BNa%2Bin%2Ban%2BElectrically%2BConductive%2BHost%2Bwith%2BPorous%2BChannels%2Bas%2Ba%2BHighly%2BStable%2BNa%2BMetal%2BAnode%26numPages%3D6%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D3797%26publicationDate%3DJune%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Composites" class="article__tags__link">Composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrochemical%20cells" class="article__tags__link">Electrochemical cells</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292007&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Wood" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Wood" class="article__tags__link">Wood</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Metallic+Na+Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Metallic%20Na%20anode">Metallic Na anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Conductive+Host&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_conductive%20host">conductive host</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Porous+Channel&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_porous%20channel">porous channel</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Low+Overpotential&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_low%20overpotential">low overpotential</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Long-term+Stability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_long-term%20stability">long-term stability</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div id="ac_i1" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h2 class="article-section__title section__title" id="_i1"> </h2><div class="NLM_p">Recently, battery technologies based on Na ion chemistry have raised great interest as alternatives to Li ion batteries, especially for grid-scale applications owing to the wide availability and sustainability advantages of Na resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1-3)</a> Over the past several years, extensive research efforts have been undertaken to develop cathode materials for Na ion batteries, which already showed great promise. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 4 to 8">(4-8)</a> As for anodes, however, the commercially available graphite failed to store enough Na ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9-11)</a> Hence, recent attempts mainly focused on the exploitation of anode materials, including carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 12 to 16">(12-16)</a> phosphorus, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17-19)</a> and alloy anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 20 to 22">(20-22)</a> Compared to other anode candidates, metallic Na shows great potential with the highest theoretical specific capacity of 1165 mA h/g and the lowest anode potential of −2.714 V vs a standard hydrogen electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> More notably, the Na metal anode is indispensable for these high-energy-density batteries where cathode materials cannot supply Na ions, such as Na–O<sub>2</sub> batteries, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 24 to 27">(24-27)</a> Na–S batteries, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 28 to 31">(28-31)</a> and Na–CO<sub>2</sub> batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32, 33)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Previous investigations have revealed that metallic anodes typically suffer from several problems in liquid electrolyte systems: (i) unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), (ii) uneven metallic deposition and dendritic growth, and (iii) large volume change upon repeated plating/stripping cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 34 to 41">(34-41)</a> These issues have been discovered as well when metallic Na was applied as anodes for Na batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref42" aria-label="reference 33 and 42">(33, 42)</a> As schematically shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, porous and dendritic Na eventually forms on bare Na anode due to the unstable SEI and the relatively infinite volume change upon long-term cycling, which have significantly prevented Na metal anode from practical applications due to the resulting low Coulombic efficiency, poor cycling performance, and safety concerns. To address these issues, Cui’s group demonstrated a compact inorganic SEI using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–glyme electrolytes, which enabled a relatively uniform and stable plating/stripping process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> A concentrated NaFSI–glyme electrolyte or NaAlCl<sub>4</sub>·2SO<sub>2</sub> inorganic electrolyte was also proposed to offer a high efficiency for metallic Na anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44, 45)</a> Our group proposed that an artificial SEI can effectively passivate the Na metal, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> which was confirmed by Sun’s group using atomic layer deposition (ALD) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> and Archer’s group with ionic liquid. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Meanwhile, inspired by a stable Li-reduced graphene oxide (Li-rGO) composite electrode, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Chen and co-workers reported a Na-rGO electrode, which exhibited great stability in Na-CO<sub>2</sub> batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> Their results suggested that confining metallic anodes in a host can greatly enhance the stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51 ref52 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 51 to 53">(51-53)</a> Despite the recent progress provided a partial solution to address the problems, research on Na metal anode is in its early stage, and developing an effective approach for highly reversible Na metal anode is still a major challenge.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01138y_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Concept of encapsulating metallic Na in carbonized wood with vertical channels as a stable anode. (a) Large volume change occurs during repeated plating/stripping cycles of planar Na electrode, leading to the formation of porous and dendritic morphology. (b) A stable Na carbonized wood (Na–wood) composite anode was achieved by restricting the repeated plating/stripping process in channels of carbonized wood.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Here, by encapsulating metallic Na into an electrically conductive host with porous channels, we propose a Na carbonized wood (Na–wood) composite electrode that tackles nearly all of these problems. Our design is inspired by the structure of natural wood, where water and ions are transported through the channels. In this case, Na plating/stripping processes are restricted in the channels of carbonized wood over long-term cycling where no amplified and uncontrolled Na deposition occur (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). In addition, the high conductive surface area would lower the effective local current density and result in a uniform Na nucleation. Benefiting from this unique structure, the Na–wood composite electrodes remain stable and spatially confined, resulting in flat plating/stripping profiles, a low overpotential of 30 mV at 0.5 mA/cm<sup>2</sup>, and superior cyclability over 500 h at 1.0 mA/cm<sup>2</sup>.</div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i2" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i2"> Results and Discussion</h3><div class="NLM_p">A generic illustration of fabricating the Na–wood composite electrode is schematically shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a. In the first step, a piece of original wood was annealed in a tube furnace under argon at 1000 °C to give the black carbonized wood (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). Encapsulation of metallic Na in the carbonized wood was accomplished by a spontaneous infusion of molten Na into the vertical channels in an argon-filled glovebox (see the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/suppl_file/nl7b01138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Experimental Section</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/suppl_file/nl7b01138_si_002.avi" class="ext-link">Movie S1</a> in the Supporting Information). The spontaneous and instantaneous infusion (about 5 s) is driven by capillary forces from the vertical-channel structured carbonized wood, which agrees with the previous observation of encapsulating molten Li or Na into the porous carbon matrix. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49 ref50 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50 ref54" aria-label="reference 49, 50 and 54">(49, 50, 54)</a> The black carbonized wood exhibited metal luster after this step, indicating the successful intake of metallic Na into these porous channels. Our approach used earth-abundant, low cost precursors and simple setup/material-processing, which offers great potential for scalable production with good reproducibility.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01138y_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Encapsulation of metallic Na into carbonized wood by a spontaneous and instantaneous infusion (5 s). (a) Schematics of the first carbonization step and the second step of infusion molten Na into porous, vertical channels to form Na-carbonized wood (Na–wood) composite electrodes. (b) Photos of a piece of wood before carbonization, after carbonization at 1000 °C, and after melt infusion of metallic Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To prove the successful encapsulation of metallic Na in carbonized wood channels, we conduct morphology analysis using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a shows a typical low-magnification SEM image of the as-prepared carbonized wood, which is composed of porous, vertical channels with channel diameters ranging from 5 to 50 μm and channel wall thickness of ∼1 μm (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). More pores can be observed on the channel walls (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c) where the hierarchical porous structure offers a larger specific area and leads to more homogeneous Na nucleation and deposition on this unique conductive skeleton by lowering the effective current density. More SEM images can be found in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/suppl_file/nl7b01138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1 and S2</a> of the Supporting Information to show the detailed structure of carbonized wood. After melt infusion of metallic Na, the channels of carbonized wood were almost fully filled, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d. A zoomed-in image reveals that the carbonized wood structure was well maintained upon the metallic Na intake (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e). EDX elemental mapping images in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f–h confirmed the SEM observations that elemental Na occupied the channels, while carbonized wood acted as a stable host. Detailed SEM and EDX mapping images of Na–wood composite in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/suppl_file/nl7b01138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3 and S4</a> indicated that metallic Na exists along the whole length of the channels spatially from bottom to top. The observation of metallic Na peaks in the X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of Na–wood composite further confirmed the encapsulation of metallic Na within the carbonized wood channels (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/suppl_file/nl7b01138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> in the Supporting Information).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01138y_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Evidence of encapsulating metallic Na in the channels of carbonized wood. (a) A low-magnification SEM image of carbonized wood. (b, c) Zoomed-in images corresponding to the area marked by red and yellow squares in panel a. (d, e) SEM images of carbonized wood after melt infusion of metallic Na, where metallic Na was encapsulated in the channels of carbonized wood as outlined by the yellow dotted line. (f–h) Corresponding Na, C, and overlapped elemental mapping images of Na–wood composite electrode indicate that channels of carbonized wood were filled up by metallic Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Prompted by this unique design, the stability of the Na–wood composite electrode is expected to improve in comparison to the bare Na metal electrode. To evaluate the electrochemical performance, 2032-type coin cells were assembled using two identical Na–wood electrodes with 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate (EC:DEC = 1:1 by volume) solution as the electrolyte. Bare Na electrodes were also employed to assemble cells as the control. The cells were first galvanostatically cycled at 0.5 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> within a protective cutoff voltage window of −3.0 to 3.0 V. Each plating/stripping step was set to be 30 min. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, the voltage profile of the Na–wood cell exhibited a small overpotential while the voltage of the bare Na cell gradually increased. Zoomed-in voltage profiles of the first 5 cycles showed that bare Na cell delivered flat plating/stripping curves as Na–wood cell in the first cycle, but dips and dumps appeared from the second cycle (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). This phenomenon has been previously observed in bare Li electrodes, which was mainly caused by the large volume change during plating/stripping. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> Such a large volume change inevitably resulted in the cracking of SEI and direct contact between newly deposited metallic Na with liquid electrolyte, which promoted the consumption of electrolyte, formation of excessive SEI, and increase of overpotential. Consequently, after cycled for less than 150 h, the bare Na cell stopped due to the voltage, reaching the protective cutoff voltage, indicative of a poor cyclic life (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). Nyquist plots confirmed the observation that the bare Na cell exhibited a small impendence after the first cycle but a much larger impendence after 100 cycles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). When the same procedure was applied to the Na–wood cell, stable plating/stripping curves with a much lower overpotential of ∼30 mV were exhibited (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes-1" aria-label="Figure S6">Figure S6</a>). This stable cycling performance is consistent with the constant impendence of the Na–wood cell (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d), indicating a great stability due to the encapsulation of metallic Na in a stable matrix.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01138y_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performance of symmetric cells using Na–wood electrodes and bare Na metal electrodes and their structural evaluation after cycling. (a) Long-term cycling performance at 0.5 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> with 30 min for each plating/stripping process and a protective cutoff voltage window of −3.0 to 3.0 V. Detailed voltage profiles of (b) the first 5 cycles and (c) marked by green rectangles in panel a. (d) EIS spectra of the Na–wood and bare Na cells collected after 1 cycle and 100 cycles. (e) SEM image of bare Na electrode after cycling (148 h), which exhibited 3D porous morphology. (f) SEM image of the Na–wood electrode after long-term cycling (250 h), showing stable morphology. (g–j) Corresponding EDX elemental mapping images of the Na–wood electrode after long-term cycling marked by yellow rectangle in panel f, where metallic Na was still confined in the channels of carbonized wood.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Since the cycling stability is closely related to the electrode structure, ex situ SEM measurements were conducted on bare Na electrode and Na–wood composite electrode after cycling. During sample preparation, it is worth noting that the bare Na cell was almost dried out after the 148-h cycling test and severe electrode pulverization occurred. The corresponding SEM image in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e demonstrated a cracked Na electrode with three-dimensional (3D) porous structures, which confirmed that the large volume change upon cycling would lead to the consumption of electrolyte and formation of porous morphology. On the other hand, metallic Na was still confined in vertical channels of carbonized wood after the long-term 250-h cycling test (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f–j). Notably, the Na–wood electrode maintained its initial structure and the Na–wood cell was still wet when it was disassembled. The significantly enhanced stability of Na–wood electrode compared to bare Na electrode proves that confinement of metallic Na in a conductive matrix and good mechanical stability of the whole composite electrode are critical for metallic anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49 ref50 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50 ref52" aria-label="reference 49, 50 and 52">(49, 50, 52)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The carbonized wood with porous channels can also function as a high-surface-area conductive host to greatly decrease the effective current density and ensure a uniform Na nucleation, affording an improved rate performance. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a and b shows the voltage profiles of Na–wood and bare Na symmetric cells cycled at 1.0 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> with 1 or 2 h for each cycle. Obviously, the Na–wood electrode exhibited stable cycling performance for more than 250 cycles at both conditions while the bare Na cell can only run for less than 90 cycles and 50 cycles. A detailed voltage profiles of Na–wood cells displayed stable plating/stripping curves with small hysteresis over hundreds of cycles (insets of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a and b). The above observations are consistent with previous reports that a conductive matrix can reduce the effective current and result in a better rate performance for both metallic Li and metallic Na. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58 ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 50 and 55 to 59">(50, 55-59)</a> It should be noted that the excellent cyclic stability of Na–wood cells was achieved in common carbonate based electrolyte system without any additives, revealing the advantages from the composite anode design. We believe that the cycling performance of the Na–wood composite electrode could be further improved using an optimized electrolyte system.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01138y_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical performance of symmetric cells using Na–wood electrodes and bare Na metal electrodes at 1.0 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> with capacity of 0.5 mAh/cm<sup>2</sup> and 1.0 mAh/cm<sup>2</sup>, respectively. Long-term cycling performance at 1.0 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> with (a) 1 h and (b) 2 h for each plating/stripping cycle. Insets are detailed voltage profiles marked by rectangles in (a) and (b).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Conclusion</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, we have demonstrated the encapsulation of metallic Na into an electrically conductive matrix with a porous channel structure that can be rapidly accomplished by a melt infusion (5 s). The protocol for fabricating Na–wood composite electrode involves only abundant precursors and simple processing. The conductive, porous framework provided by the carbonized wood offers an ideal host for metallic Na during electrochemical plating/stripping cycles, which enables much improved cycling performance by its good mechanical stability and confinement of metallic Na. Furthermore, the rate performance is enhanced by lowering the effective current density and ensuring a uniform Na nucleation due to the high conductive surface area. We believe that this unique design promises a potential solution for metallic Na anode and can also be extended to other metallic anodes for multiple applications.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138" class="ext-link">10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Details for experimental and characterizations; Figures S1–S6 of SEM images of carbonized wood, SEM and EDX elemental mapping images of Na–wood electrode, XRD patterns of carbonized wood and Na–wood electrode, detailed voltage profiles of a symmetric cell using Na–wood electrodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/suppl_file/nl7b01138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li><li><p class="inline">Movie S1 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/suppl_file/nl7b01138_si_002.avi" class="ext-link">AVI</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/suppl_file/nl7b01138_si_001.pdf">nl7b01138_si_001.pdf (4.69 MB)</a></li><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/suppl_file/nl7b01138_si_002.avi">nl7b01138_si_002.avi (15.45 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New results about the sodium-graphite system</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Metrot, A.; Guerard, D.; Billaud, D.; Herold, A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Synthetic Metals</span>

                        cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljTAb_-Dr25-w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1149/1.1379565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stevens, D.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The mechanisms of lithium and sodium insertion in carbon materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Stevens, D. A.; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors compare the interactions of lithium and sodium with a range of carbon materials in electrochem. cells.  Through wide angle in situ x-ray scattering studies, both lithium and sodium can be inserted into the interlayer space in disordered carbon materials.  This insertion process is accompanied by an increase in the interlayer spacing in these materials.  Small-angle in situ scattering studies are presented to clearly show the insertion of lithium and sodium into nanopores within disordered hard carbons.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This article proposes a strategy by designing a sandwich-like nanocomposite to overcome the disadvantages of the non-graphitized carbon materials.  This article summarizes that the developed a facile ionothermal method to synthesize sandwich-like hierarchically porous carbon/graphene composite, in which hierarchical porous carbon facilitates Na+ insertion and graphene guarantees the high electronic cond. for electrons supply.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrul7DVrUFn6rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljTAb_-Dr25-w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXpt1Wjurk%253D&amp;md5=3dec7b7bfc133e4038c2ad0de8f47e12</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201601602"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ye, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Deng, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zeng, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nie, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Duchesne, P.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A phosphorene-graphene hybrid material as a high-capacity anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Jie; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Pasta, Mauro; Yuan, Hongtao; Zheng, Guangyuan; Sun, Yongming; Li, Yuzhang; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">980-985</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have recently attracted significant attention as an alternative to Li-ion batteries because Na sources do not present the geopolitical issues that Li sources might.  Although recent reports on cathode materials for Na-ion batteries demonstrated performances comparable to their Li-ion counterparts, the major scientific challenge for a competitive Na-ion battery technol. is to develop viable anode materials.  A hybrid material made out of a few phosphorene layers sandwiched between graphene layers shows a specific capacity of 2 440 mA h g-1 (calcd. using the mass of P only) at a c.d. of 0.05 A g-1 and an 83% capacity retention after 100 cycles while operating between 0 and 1.5 V.  Using in situ TEM and ex situ x-ray diffraction techniques, the authors explain the large capacity of the anode through a dual mechanism of intercalation of Na ions along the x axis of the phosphorene layers followed by the formation of a Na3P alloy.  The presence of graphene layers in the hybrid material works as a mech. backbone and an elec. highway, ensuring that a suitable elastic buffer space accommodates the anisotropic expansion of phosphorene layers along the y and z axial directions for stable cycling operation.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp8p8kp9p5tl7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljTAb_-Dr25-w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsV2ltrrM&amp;md5=23b94375b97b50394801c280ff017cb8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201209689"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Angew.                 10.1038/ncomms11722</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DWei%252C%2BS.%253B%2BXu%252C%2BS.%253B%2BAgrawral%252C%2BA.%253B%2BChoudhury%252C%2BS.%253B%2BLu%252C%2BY.%253B%2BTu%252C%2BZ.%253B%2BMa%252C%2BL.%253B%2BArcher%252C%2BL.%2BA.Nat.%2BCommun.%2B2016%252C%2B7%252C%2B11722&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fncomms11722&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A stable room-temperature sodium-sulfur battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Shuya; Xu, Shaomao; Agrawral, Akanksha; Choudhury, Snehashis; Lu, Yingying; Tu, Zhengyuan; Ma, Lin; Archer, Lynden A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11722</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-energy rechargeable batteries based on earth-abundant materials are important for mobile and stationary storage technologies.  Rechargeable sodium-sulfur batteries able to operate stably at room temp. are among the most sought-after platforms because such cells take advantage of a two-electron-redox process to achieve high storage capacity from inexpensive electrode materials.  Here we report a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery that uses a microporous carbon-sulfur composite cathode, and a liq. carbonate electrolyte contg. the ionic liq. 1-methyl-3-propylimidazolium-chlorate tethered to SiO2 nanoparticles.  We show that these cells can cycle stably at a rate of 0.5 C (1 C=1675, mAh g-1) with 600 mAh g-1 reversible capacity and nearly 100% Coulombic efficiency.  By means of spectroscopic and electrochem. anal., we find that the particles form a sodium-ion conductive film on the anode, which stabilizes deposition of sodium.  We also find that sulfur remains interred in the carbon pores and undergo solid-state electrochem. reactions with sodium ions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdxq-Lo84ftbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljEPB6gvY_zoQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xpslymsb4%253D&amp;md5=41798f2b5b34a2e7fda7224a8c7d4100</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201403257"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Manthiram, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, X.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Small</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">11</span><span class="NLM_x"> (</span><span class="NLM_issue">18</span><span class="NLM_x">) </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">2108</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">2114</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Selective deposition and stable encapsulation of lithium through heterogeneous seeded growth</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yan, Kai; Lu, Zhenda; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Xiong, Feng; Hsu, Po-Chun; Li, Yuzhang; Zhao, Jie; Chu, Steven; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Energy</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal is an attractive anode material for rechargeable batteries, owing to its high theor. specific capacity of 3,860 mAh g-1.  Despite extensive research efforts, there are still many fundamental challenges in using lithium metal in lithium-ion batteries.  Most notably, crit. information such as its nucleation and growth behavior remains elusive.  Here we explore the nucleation pattern of lithium on various metal substrates and unravel a substrate-dependent growth phenomenon that enables selective deposition of lithium metal.  With the aid of binary phase diagrams, we find that no nucleation barriers are present for metals exhibiting a definite soly. in lithium, whereas appreciable nucleation barriers exist for metals with negligible soly.  We thereafter design a nanocapsule structure for lithium metal anodes consisting of hollow carbon spheres with nanoparticle seeds inside.  During deposition, the lithium metal is found to predominantly grow inside the hollow carbon spheres.                         cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium metal anodes for rechargeable batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Wu; Wang, Jiulin; Ding, Fei; Chen, Xilin; Nasybulin, Eduard; Zhang, Yaohui; Zhang, Ji-Guang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
