            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li) metal is an ideal anode material for rechargeable batteries due to its extremely high theor. specific capacity (3860 mA h g-1), low d. (0.59 g cm-3) and the lowest neg. electrochem. potential (-3.040 V vs. the std. hydrogen electrode).  Unfortunately, uncontrollable dendritic Li growth and limited Coulombic efficiency during Li deposition/stripping inherent in these batteries have prevented their practical applications over the past 40 years.  With the emergence of post-Li-ion batteries, safe and efficient operation of Li metal anodes has become an enabling technol. which may det. the fate of several promising candidates for the next generation energy storage systems, including rechargeable Li-air batteries, Li-S batteries, and Li metal batteries which utilize intercalation compds. as cathodes.  In this paper, various factors that affect the morphol. and Coulombic efficiency of Li metal anodes have been analyzed.  Technologies utilized to characterize the morphol. of Li deposition and the results obtained by modeling of Li dendrite growth have also been reviewed.  Finally, recent development and urgent need in this field are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoNGXuk9rqhzrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgeTQPKTQf1sg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhsFaktL8%253D&amp;md5=3813f919eb7ca4218cfb562f54c9382a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201500213"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cheng, X.-B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, C.-Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wei, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, J.-G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Q.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit37R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">37</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Review of Solid Electrolyte Interphases on Lithium Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cheng Xin-Bing; Zhang Rui; Zhao Chen-Zi; Wei Fei; Zhang Qiang; Zhang Ji-Guang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1500213</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2198-3844</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal batteries (LMBs) are among the most promising candidates of high-energy-density devices for advanced energy storage.  However, the growth of dendrites greatly hinders the practical applications of LMBs in portable electronics and electric vehicles.  Constructing stable and efficient solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) is among the most effective strategies to inhibit the dendrite growth and thus to achieve a superior cycling performance.  In this review, the mechanisms of SEI formation and models of SEI structure are briefly summarized.  The analysis methods to probe the surface chemistry, surface morphology, electrochemical property, dynamic characteristics of SEI layer are emphasized.  The critical factors affecting the SEI formation, such as electrolyte component, temperature, current density, are comprehensively debated.  The efficient methods to modify SEI layer with the introduction of new electrolyte system and additives, ex-situ-formed protective layer, as well as electrode design, are summarized.  Although these works afford new insights into SEI research, robust and precise routes for SEI modification with well-designed structure, as well as understanding of the connection between structure and electrochemical performance, is still inadequate.  A multidisciplinary approach is highly required to enable the formation of robust SEI for highly efficient energy storage systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRKexG6j4P6cLqxQ2mb9Pu8fW6udTcc2eYhftT3jU5fSbntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC2srlsVymtw%253D%253D&amp;md5=4e2625588d90c05dc5bf55d7bc68bd68</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1038/nnano.2014.152"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, S.                         cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interconnected hollow carbon nanospheres for stable lithium metal anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zheng, Guangyuan; Lee, Seok Woo; Liang, Zheng; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Yan, Kai; Yao, Hongbin; Wang, Haotian; Li, Weiyang; Chu, Steven; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">618-623</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">For future applications in portable electronics, elec. vehicles and grid storage, batteries with higher energy storage d. than existing lithium ion batteries need to be developed.  Recent efforts in this direction have focused on high-capacity electrode materials such as lithium metal, silicon and tin as anodes, and sulfur and oxygen as cathodes.  Lithium metal would be the optimal choice as an anode material, because it has the highest specific capacity (3,860 mAh g-1) and the lowest anode potential of all.  However, the lithium anode forms dendritic and mossy metal deposits, leading to serious safety concerns and low Coulombic efficiency during charge/discharge cycles.  Although advanced characterization techniques have helped shed light on the lithium growth process, effective strategies to improve lithium metal anode cycling remain elusive.  Here, we show that coating the lithium metal anode with a monolayer of interconnected amorphous hollow carbon nanospheres helps isolate the lithium metal depositions and facilitates the formation of a stable solid electrolyte interphase.  We show that lithium dendrites do not form up to a practical c.d. of 1 mA cm-2.  The Coulombic efficiency improves to ∼99% for more than 150 cycles.  This is significantly better than the bare unmodified samples, which usually show rapid Coulombic efficiency decay in fewer than 100 cycles.  Our results indicate that nanoscale interfacial engineering could be a promising strategy to tackle the intrinsic problems of lithium metal anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqYo_CVpJyG5LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhjYsCR1hvpng"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advanced Micro/Nanostructures for Lithium Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang Rui; Cheng Xin-Bing; Zhang Qiang; Li Nian-Wu; Yin Ya-Xia; Guo Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1600445</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2198-3844</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Owning to their very high theoretical capacity, lithium metal anodes are expected to fuel the extensive practical applications in portable electronics and electric vehicles.  However, unstable solid electrolyte interphase and lithium dendrite growth during lithium plating/stripping induce poor safety, low Coulombic efficiency, and short span life of lithium metal batteries.  Lately, varies of micro/nanostructured lithium metal anodes are proposed to address these issues in lithium metal batteries.  With the unique surface, pore, and connecting structures of different nanomaterials, lithium plating/stripping processes have been regulated.  Thus the electrochemical properties and lithium morphologies have been significantly improved.  These micro/nanostructured lithium metal anodes shed new light on the future applications for lithium metal batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRzOrk04pyNfuRS7coPn-RtfW6udTcc2eYhftT3jU5fSbntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1cvgtVOhtA%253D%253D&amp;md5=54f2488c1289447fb8f0837cf80d19b8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1039/C5CS00410A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Seh, Z.                         cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Highly Reversible Room-Temperature Sodium Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Seh, Zhi Wei; Sun, Jie; Sun, Yongming; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Central Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">449-455</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACSCII</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2374-7951</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to its low cost and high natural abundance, sodium metal is among the most promising anode materials for energy storage technologies beyond lithium ion batteries.  However, room-temp. sodium metal anodes suffer from poor reversibility during long-term plating and stripping, mainly due to formation of nonuniform solid electrolyte interphase as well as dendritic growth of sodium metal.  Herein it is reported for the first time that a simple liq. electrolyte, sodium hexafluorophosphate in glymes (mono-, di-, and tetraglyme), can enable highly reversible and nondendritic plating-stripping of sodium metal anodes at room temp.  High av. Coulombic efficiencies of 99.9% were achieved over 300 plating-stripping cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2.  The long-term reversibility was found to arise from the formation of a uniform, inorg. solid electrolyte interphase made of sodium oxide and sodium fluoride, which is highly impermeable to electrolyte solvent and conducive to nondendritic growth.  As a proof of concept, a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery is demonstrated using this class of electrolytes, paving the way for the development of next-generation, sodium-based energy storage technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdqXOWzEW74bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljJVryAK1y0DQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVGmtrfL&amp;md5=6155e3c781a24b8340081c2ad4444616</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.09.013"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mishra, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Engelhard, M.                         cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Layered reduced graphene oxide with nanoscale interlayer gaps as a stable host for lithium metal anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lin, Dingchang; Liu, Yayuan; Liang, Zheng; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Sun, Jie; Wang, Haotian; Yan, Kai; Xie, Jin; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">626-632</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Metallic lithium is a promising anode candidate for future high-energy-d. lithium batteries.  It is a light-wt. material, and has the highest theor. capacity (3,860 mAh g-1) and the lowest electrochem. potential of all candidates.  There are, however, at least three major hurdles before lithium metal anodes can become a viable technol.: uneven and dendritic lithium deposition, unstable solid electrolyte interphase and almost infinite relative dimension change during cycling.  Previous research has tackled the first two issues, but the last is still mostly unsolved.  Here we report a composite lithium metal anode that exhibits low dimension variation (∼20%) during cycling and good mech. flexibility.  The anode is composed of 7 wt% 'lithiophilic' layered reduced graphene oxide with nanoscale gaps that can host metallic lithium.  The anode retains up to ∼3,390 mAh/g of capacity, exhibits low overpotential (∼80 mV at 3 mA/cm2) and a flat voltage profile in a carbonate electrolyte.  A full-cell battery with a LiCoO2 cathode shows good rate capability and flat voltage profiles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpsfxppz9W8KrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljJVryAK1y0DQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XksFKqsLg%253D&amp;md5=29b616312d14b4db2df7d896cba51506</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1126/sciadv.1602396"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tao, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, J.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Sci.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms10992"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lin, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cui, Y.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat.                         cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium-coated polymeric matrix as a minimum volume-change and dendrite-free lithium metal anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yayuan; Lin, Dingchang; Liang, Zheng; Zhao, Jie; Yan, Kai; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10992</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal is the ideal anode for the next generation of high-energy-d. batteries.  Nevertheless, dendrite growth, side reactions and infinite relative vol. change have prevented it from practical applications.  Here, we demonstrate a promising metallic lithium anode design by infusing molten lithium into a polymeric matrix.  The electrospun polyimide employed is stable against highly reactive molten lithium and, via a conformal layer of zinc oxide coating to render the surface lithiophilic, molten lithium can be drawn into the matrix, affording a nano-porous lithium electrode.  Importantly, the polymeric backbone enables uniform lithium stripping/plating, which successfully confines lithium within the matrix, realizing min. vol. change and effective dendrite suppression.  The porous electrode reduces the effective c.d.; thus, flat voltage profiles and stable cycling of more than 100 cycles is achieved even at a high c.d. of 5 mA cm-2 in both carbonate and ether electrolyte.  The advantages of the porous, polymeric matrix provide important insights into the design principles of lithium metal anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpiFQKrkZVOgrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljJVryAK1y0DQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xks1ert7s%253D&amp;md5=665ac781f0a668a2caa866fa352b55d3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit52" data-doi="10.1073/pnas.1518188113"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lin, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tao, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cui, Y.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Proc.                             cas_R

D.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Today</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">7</span><span class="NLM_x"> (</span><span class="NLM_issue">1</span><span class="NLM_x">) </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">10</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">20</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.nantod.2011.11.002</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D10-20%26journal%3DNano%2BToday%26issue%3D1%26author%3DX.%2BJi%26author%3DD.-Y.%2BLiu%26author%3DD.%2BG.%2BPrendiville%26author%3DY.%2BZhang%26author%3DX.%2BLiu%26author%3DG.%2BD.%2BStucky%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.nantod.2011.11.002&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.nantod.2011.11.002&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Spatially heterogeneous carbon-fiber papers as surface dendrite-free current collectors for lithium deposition</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ji, Xiulei; Liu, De-Yu; Prendiville, Daniel G.; Zhang, Yichi; Liu, Xiaonao; Stucky, Galen D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Today</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10-20</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NTAOCG</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-0132</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Summary: Despite the high energy d., the lithium metal electrode has been plagued for decades with a dendrite growth problem that can result in a battery thermal runaway.  Here, we introduce anisotropic spatially heterogeneous three dimensional (3D) current collectors that prevent lithium deposition on the insulating electrolyte-facing surface and accommodate lithium deposition inside the spacious voids.  The anisotropic spatial heterogeneity was introduced by a line-of-sight deposition of a thin SiO2 layer onto a carbon-fiber paper.  The deposited SiO2 was later converted into SiC by a magnesiothermic reaction.  The SiO2 and SiC decorated 3D current collectors were confirmed dendrite-free by ex situ SEM observation after a deep lithium deposition of 28.8 C cm-2 at a high c.d. of 4 mA cm-2.  A high lithium cycling efficiency of 94% was achieved over deep deposition (14.4 C cm-2) and stripping cycles in a carbonate based org. electrolyte, demonstrating the superiority of the novel current collector for dendrite control and lithium cycling.  This strategy opens new avenues to address the dendrite problem by rationally designed current collectors and for the creation of a high energy d. electrode.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoJR14_dU_WgbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgy129RuL7uzg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38Xjt1Omtb0%253D&amp;md5=fe8ee8bead3d97a9c0eae2344d4a77e7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit58" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms9058"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, C.-P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yin, Y.-X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, S.-F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, N.-W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, Y.-G.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat. Commun.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">6</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">8058</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/ncomms9058</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DYang%252C%2BC.-P.%253B%2BYin%252C%2BY.-X.%253B%2BZhang%252C%2BS.-F.%253B%2BLi%252C%2BN.-W.%253B%2BGuo%252C%2BY.-G.Nat.%2BCommun.%2B2015%252C%2B6%252C%2B8058&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fncomms9058&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit58R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">58</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Accommodating lithium into 3D current collectors with a submicron skeleton towards long-life lithium metal anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Chun-Peng; Yin, Ya-Xia; Zhang, Shuai-Feng; Li, Nian-Wu; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">8058</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal is one of the most attractive anode materials for electrochem. energy storage.  However, the growth of Li dendrites during electrochem. deposition, which leads to a low Coulombic efficiency and safety concerns, has long hindered the application of rechargeable Li-metal batteries.  Here we show that a 3D current collector with a submicron skeleton and high electroactive surface area can significantly improve the electrochem. deposition behavior of Li.  Li anode is accommodated in the 3D structure without uncontrollable Li dendrites.  With the growth of Li dendrites being effectively suppressed, the Li anode in the 3D current collector can run for 600 h without short circuit and exhibits low voltage hysteresis.  The exceptional electrochem. performance of the Li-metal anode in the 3D current collector highlights the importance of rational design of current collectors and reveals a new avenue for developing Li anodes with a long lifespan.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq5vVAff3FKwLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgy129RuL7uzg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsVKhurnM&amp;md5=8f3dc7915f87598e9c930e252db773a8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref59"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01138y_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0001.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr1" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Concept of encapsulating metallic Na in carbonized wood with vertical channels as a stable anode. (a) Large volume change occurs during repeated plating/stripping cycles of planar Na electrode, leading to the formation of porous and dendritic morphology. (b) A stable Na carbonized wood (Na–wood) composite anode was achieved by restricting the repeated plating/stripping process in channels of carbonized wood.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01138y_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0002.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr2" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Encapsulation of metallic Na into carbonized wood by a spontaneous and instantaneous infusion (5 s). (a) Schematics of the first carbonization step and the second step of infusion molten Na into porous, vertical channels to form Na-carbonized wood (Na–wood) composite electrodes. (b) Photos of a piece of wood before carbonization, after carbonization at 1000 °C, and after melt infusion of metallic Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01138y_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0003.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr3" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Evidence of encapsulating metallic Na in the channels of carbonized wood. (a) A low-magnification SEM image of carbonized wood. (b, c) Zoomed-in images corresponding to the area marked by red and yellow squares in panel a. (d, e) SEM images of carbonized wood after melt infusion of metallic Na, where metallic Na was encapsulated in the channels of carbonized wood as outlined by the yellow dotted line. (f–h) Corresponding Na, C, and overlapped elemental mapping images of Na–wood composite electrode indicate that channels of carbonized wood were filled up by metallic Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01138y_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0004.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr4" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performance of symmetric cells using Na–wood electrodes and bare Na metal electrodes and their structural evaluation after cycling. (a) Long-term cycling performance at 0.5 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> with 30 min for each plating/stripping process and a protective cutoff voltage window of −3.0 to 3.0 V. Detailed voltage profiles of (b) the first 5 cycles and (c) marked by green rectangles in panel a. (d) EIS spectra of the Na–wood and bare Na cells collected after 1 cycle and 100 cycles. (e) SEM image of bare Na electrode after cycling (148 h), which exhibited 3D porous morphology. (f) SEM image of the Na–wood electrode after long-term cycling (250 h), showing stable morphology. (g–j) Corresponding EDX elemental mapping images of the Na–wood electrode after long-term cycling marked by yellow rectangle in panel f, where metallic Na was still confined in the channels of carbonized wood.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig5"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01138y_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0005.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr5" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical performance of symmetric cells using Na–wood electrodes and bare Na metal electrodes at 1.0 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> with capacity of 0.5 mAh/cm<sup>2</sup> and 1.0 mAh/cm<sup>2</sup>, respectively. Long-term cycling performance at 1.0 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> with (a) 1 h and (b) 2 h for each plating/stripping cycle. Insets are detailed voltage profiles marked by rectangles in (a) and (b).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01138y_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01138&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></li><li id="pane-m2" aria-labelledby="pane-m2" class="tab__pane tab__pane-references"><div class="refs-header-label"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i7">References</h2></div></div></div><hr></div><p class="rightTab-references-count">

The interplanar distance between 2 C layers surrounding a Na plane is 4.52 Å.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoPLvG0fit7t7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLPzlevrmxIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaL3cXmsV2hu7Y%253D&amp;md5=7ed534de159c05fbc0aadb6581db487f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shen, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bommier, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, L.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Acc. Chem. Res.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">49</span><span class="NLM_x"> (</span><span class="NLM_issue">2</span><span class="NLM_x">) </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">231</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">240</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLPzlevrmxIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1149/1.1379565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stevens, D. A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J. R.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Electrochem. Soc.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2001</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">148</span><span class="NLM_x"> (</span><span class="NLM_issue">8</span><span class="NLM_x">) </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">A803</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">A811</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/1.1379565</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The mechanisms of lithium and sodium insertion in carbon materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Stevens, D. A.; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2001</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">148</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A803-A811</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors compare the interactions of lithium and sodium with a range of carbon materials in electrochem. cells.  Through wide angle in situ x-ray scattering studies, both lithium and sodium can be inserted into the interlayer space in disordered carbon materials.  This insertion process is accompanied by an increase in the interlayer spacing in these materials.  Small-angle in situ scattering studies are presented to clearly show the insertion of lithium and sodium into nanopores within disordered hard carbons.  Also very little, if any, sodium can be inserted into graphitic materials in contrast to the large capacity seen for lithium insertion.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqOuo4QAURxurVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLPzlevrmxIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXlvFKntr0%253D&amp;md5=15fc03ac3de24271573ed94315375242</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref12" class="rightTabRefNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit12" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393348"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stevens, D.                 10.1038/nnano.2015.194</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A phosphorene-graphene hybrid material as a high-capacity anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Jie; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Pasta, Mauro; Yuan, Hongtao; Zheng, Guangyuan; Sun, Yongming; Li, Yuzhang; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">980-985</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have recently attracted significant attention as an alternative to Li-ion batteries because Na sources do not present the geopolitical issues that Li sources might.  Although recent reports on cathode materials for Na-ion batteries demonstrated performances comparable to their Li-ion counterparts, the major scientific challenge for a competitive Na-ion battery technol. is to develop viable anode materials.  A hybrid material made out of a few phosphorene layers sandwiched between graphene layers shows a specific capacity of 2 440 mA h g-1 (calcd. using the mass of P only) at a c.d. of 0.05 A g-1 and an 83% capacity retention after 100 cycles while operating between 0 and 1.5 V.  Using in situ TEM and ex situ x-ray diffraction techniques, the authors explain the large capacity of the anode through a dual mechanism of intercalation of Na ions along the x axis of the phosphorene layers followed by the formation of a Na3P alloy.  The presence of graphene layers in the hybrid material works as a mech. backbone and an elec. highway, ensuring that a suitable elastic buffer space accommodates the anisotropic expansion of phosphorene layers along the y and z axial directions for stable cycling operation.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp8p8kp9p5tl7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLPzlevrmxIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsV2ltrrM&amp;md5=23b94375b97b50394801c280ff017cb8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref19" class="rightTabRefNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit19" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201209689"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Angew.                 10.1038/ncomms11722</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A stable room-temperature sodium-sulfur battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Shuya; Xu, Shaomao; Agrawral, Akanksha; Choudhury, Snehashis; Lu, Yingying; Tu, Zhengyuan; Ma, Lin; Archer, Lynden A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11722</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-energy rechargeable batteries based on earth-abundant materials are important for mobile and stationary storage technologies.  Rechargeable sodium-sulfur batteries able to operate stably at room temp. are among the most sought-after platforms because such cells take advantage of a two-electron-redox process to achieve high storage capacity from inexpensive electrode materials.  Here we report a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery that uses a microporous carbon-sulfur composite cathode, and a liq. carbonate electrolyte contg. the ionic liq. 1-methyl-3-propylimidazolium-chlorate tethered to SiO2 nanoparticles.  We show that these cells can cycle stably at a rate of 0.5 C (1 C=1675, mAh g-1) with 600 mAh g-1 reversible capacity and nearly 100% Coulombic efficiency.  By means of spectroscopic and electrochem. anal., we find that the particles form a sodium-ion conductive film on the anode, which stabilizes deposition of sodium.  We also find that sulfur remains interred in the carbon pores and undergo solid-state electrochem. reactions with sodium ions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdxq-Lo84ftbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgKlfXY_B6M8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xpslymsb4%253D&amp;md5=41798f2b5b34a2e7fda7224a8c7d4100</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref30" class="rightTabRefNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit30" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201403257"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Manthiram, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, X.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Small</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">11</span><span class="NLM_x"> (</span><span class="NLM_issue">18</span><span class="NLM_x">) </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">2108</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">2114</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                10.1039/C4TA04130E</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A rechargeable Na-CO2/O2 battery enabled by stable nanoparticle hybrid electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Shaomao; Lu, Yingying; Wang, Hongsen; Abruna, Hector D.; Archer, Lynden A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">42</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">17723-17729</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report on rechargeable batteries that use metallic sodium as the anode, a mixt. of CO2 and O2 as the active material in the cathode, and an org.-inorg. hybrid liq. as electrolyte.  The batteries are attractive among energy storage technologies because they provide a mechanism for simultaneously capturing CO2 emissions while generating elec. energy.  Through in and ex situ chem. anal. of the cathode we show that NaHCO3 is the principal discharge product, and that its relative instability permits cell recharging.  By means of differential electrochem. mass spectrometry (DEMS) based on 12C and 13C we further show that addn. of as little as 10% of 1-methyl-3-propylimidazolium bis(trifluoromethanesulfone)imide ionic liq. tethered to SiO2 nanoparticles extends the high-voltage stability of the electrolyte by at least 1 V, allowing recharge of the Na-CO2/O2 cells.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqiEKp9pxRQKLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgKlfXY_B6M8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhsFamtL%252FN&amp;md5=de6567e1523f52b9d1c56d6d89b95710</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201602504"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, J.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Angew.     <span class="NLM_cas:pages">16010</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal is an attractive anode material for rechargeable batteries, owing to its high theor. specific capacity of 3,860 mAh g-1.  Despite extensive research efforts, there are still many fundamental challenges in using lithium metal in lithium-ion batteries.  Most notably, crit. information such as its nucleation and growth behavior remains elusive.  Here we explore the nucleation pattern of lithium on various metal substrates and unravel a substrate-dependent growth phenomenon that enables selective deposition of lithium metal.  With the aid of binary phase diagrams, we find that no nucleation barriers are present for metals exhibiting a definite soly. in lithium, whereas appreciable nucleation barriers exist for metals with negligible soly.  We thereafter design a nanocapsule structure for lithium metal anodes consisting of hollow carbon spheres with nanoparticle seeds inside.  During deposition, the lithium metal is found to predominantly grow inside the hollow carbon spheres.  Such selective deposition and stable encapsulation of lithium metal eliminate dendrite formation and enable improved cycling, even in corrosive alkyl carbonate electrolytes, with 98% coulombic efficiency for more than 300 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrPXEqfqfc41LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgKlfXY_B6M8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVekur4%253D&amp;md5=1efe7249d8c35f8ceb20beb6dd70f69c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref36" class="rightTabRefNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit36" data-doi="10.1039/C3EE40795K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nasybulin, E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, J.-G.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ.                         rightTab-cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium metal anodes for rechargeable batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Wu; Wang, Jiulin; Ding, Fei; Chen, Xilin; Nasybulin, Eduard; Zhang, Yaohui; Zhang, Ji-Guang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li) metal is an ideal anode material for rechargeable batteries due to its extremely high theor. specific capacity (3860 mA h g-1), low d. (0.59 g cm-3) and the lowest neg. electrochem. potential (-3.040 V vs. the std. hydrogen electrode).  Unfortunately, uncontrollable dendritic Li growth and limited Coulombic efficiency during Li deposition/stripping inherent in these batteries have prevented their practical applications over the past 40 years.  With the emergence of post-Li-ion batteries, safe and efficient operation of Li metal anodes has become an enabling technol. which may det. the fate of several promising candidates for the next generation energy storage systems, including rechargeable Li-air batteries, Li-S batteries, and Li metal batteries which utilize intercalation compds. as cathodes.  In this paper, various factors that affect the morphol. and Coulombic efficiency of Li metal anodes have been analyzed.  Technologies utilized to characterize the morphol. of Li deposition and the results obtained by modeling of Li dendrite growth have also been reviewed.  Finally, recent development and urgent need in this field are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoNGXuk9rqhzrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgKlfXY_B6M8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhsFaktL8%253D&amp;md5=3813f919eb7ca4218cfb562f54c9382a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref37" class="rightTabRefNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit37" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201500213"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cheng, X.-B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, C.-Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wei, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, J.-G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Q.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         rightTab-cas_cit37R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">37</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Review of Solid Electrolyte Interphases on Lithium Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cheng Xin-Bing; Zhang Rui; Zhao Chen-Zi; Wei Fei; Zhang Qiang; Zhang Ji-Guang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal batteries (LMBs) are among the most promising candidates of high-energy-density devices for advanced energy storage.  However, the growth of dendrites greatly hinders the practical applications of LMBs in portable electronics and electric vehicles.  Constructing stable and efficient solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) is among the most effective strategies to inhibit the dendrite growth and thus to achieve a superior cycling performance.  In this review, the mechanisms of SEI formation and models of SEI structure are briefly summarized.  The analysis methods to probe the surface chemistry, surface morphology, electrochemical property, dynamic characteristics of SEI layer are emphasized.  The critical factors affecting the SEI formation, such as electrolyte component, temperature, current density, are comprehensively debated.  The efficient methods to modify SEI layer with the introduction of new electrolyte system and additives, ex-situ-formed protective layer, as well as electrode design, are summarized.  Although these works afford new insights into SEI research, robust and precise routes for SEI modification with well-designed structure, as well as understanding of the connection between structure and electrochemical performance, is still inadequate.  A multidisciplinary approach is highly required to enable the formation of robust SEI for highly efficient energy storage systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRKexG6j4P6cLqxQ2mb9Pu8fW6udTcc2eaNetk98vO2ILntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC2srlsVymtw%253D%253D&amp;md5=4e2625588d90c05dc5bf55d7bc68bd68</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref38" class="rightTabRefNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit38" data-doi="10.1038/nnano.2014.152"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, S.                         rightTab-cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interconnected hollow carbon nanospheres for stable lithium metal anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zheng, Guangyuan; Lee, Seok Woo; Liang, Zheng; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Yan, Kai; Yao, Hongbin; Wang, Haotian; Li, Weiyang; Chu, Steven; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">618-623</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">For future applications in portable electronics, elec. vehicles and grid storage, batteries with higher energy storage d. than existing lithium ion batteries need to be developed.  Recent efforts in this direction have focused on high-capacity electrode materials such as lithium metal, silicon and tin as anodes, and sulfur and oxygen as cathodes.  Lithium metal would be the optimal choice as an anode material, because it has the highest specific capacity (3,860 mAh g-1) and the lowest anode potential of all.  However, the lithium anode forms dendritic and mossy metal deposits, leading to serious safety concerns and low Coulombic efficiency during charge/discharge cycles.  Although advanced characterization techniques have helped shed light on the lithium growth process, effective strategies to improve lithium metal anode cycling remain elusive.  Here, we show that coating the lithium metal anode with a monolayer of interconnected amorphous hollow carbon nanospheres helps isolate the lithium metal depositions and facilitates the formation of a stable solid electrolyte interphase.  We show that lithium dendrites do not form up to a practical c.d. of 1 mA cm-2.  The Coulombic efficiency improves to ∼99% for more than 150 cycles.  This is significantly better than the bare unmodified samples, which usually show rapid Coulombic efficiency decay in fewer than 100 cycles.  Our results indicate that nanoscale interfacial engineering could be a promising strategy to tackle the intrinsic problems of lithium metal anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqYo_CVpJyG5LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljTo2vFzgV48A"> &gt;&gt;                         rightTab-cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advanced Micro/Nanostructures for Lithium Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang Rui; Cheng Xin-Bing; Zhang Qiang; Li Nian-Wu; Yin Ya-Xia; Guo Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Owning to their very high theoretical capacity, lithium metal anodes are expected to fuel the extensive practical applications in portable electronics and electric vehicles.                         rightTab-cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Highly Reversible Room-Temperature Sodium Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Seh, Zhi Wei; Sun, Jie; Sun, Yongming; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Central Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">449-455</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to its low cost and high natural abundance, sodium metal is among the most promising anode materials for energy storage technologies beyond lithium ion batteries.  However, room-temp. sodium metal anodes suffer from poor reversibility during long-term plating and stripping, mainly due to formation of nonuniform solid electrolyte interphase as well as dendritic growth of sodium metal.  Herein it is reported for the first time that a simple liq. electrolyte, sodium hexafluorophosphate in glymes (mono-, di-, and tetraglyme), can enable highly reversible and nondendritic plating-stripping of sodium metal anodes at room temp.  High av. Coulombic efficiencies of 99.9% were achieved over 300 plating-stripping cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2.  The long-term reversibility was found to arise from the formation of a uniform, inorg. solid electrolyte interphase made of sodium oxide and sodium fluoride, which is highly impermeable to electrolyte solvent and conducive to nondendritic growth.  As a proof of concept, a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery is demonstrated using this class of electrolytes, paving the way for the development of next-generation, sodium-based energy storage technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdqXOWzEW74bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljTo2vFzgV48A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVGmtrfL&amp;md5=6155e3c781a24b8340081c2ad4444616</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.09.013"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mishra, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Engelhard, M.                 10.1038/nnano.2016.32</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Layered reduced graphene oxide with nanoscale interlayer gaps as a stable host for lithium metal anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lin, Dingchang; Liu, Yayuan; Liang, Zheng; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Sun, Jie; Wang, Haotian; Yan, Kai; Xie, Jin; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">626-632</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Metallic lithium is a promising anode candidate for future high-energy-d. lithium batteries.  It is a light-wt. material, and has the highest theor. capacity (3,860 mAh g-1) and the lowest electrochem. potential of all candidates.  There are, however, at least three major hurdles before lithium metal anodes can become a viable technol.: uneven and dendritic lithium deposition, unstable solid electrolyte interphase and almost infinite relative dimension change during cycling.  Previous research has tackled the first two issues, but the last is still mostly unsolved.  Here we report a composite lithium metal anode that exhibits low dimension variation (∼20%) during cycling and good mech. flexibility.  The anode is composed of 7 wt% 'lithiophilic' layered reduced graphene oxide with nanoscale gaps that can host metallic lithium.  The anode retains up to ∼3,390 mAh/g of capacity, exhibits low overpotential (∼80 mV at 3 mA/cm2) and a flat voltage profile in a carbonate electrolyte.  A full-cell battery with a LiCoO2 cathode shows good rate capability and flat voltage profiles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpsfxppz9W8KrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljTo2vFzgV48A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XksFKqsLg%253D&amp;md5=29b616312d14b4db2df7d896cba51506</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref50" class="rightTabRefNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit50" data-doi="10.1126/sciadv.1602396"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tao, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, J.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Sci. Commun.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">7</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">10992</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/ncomms10992</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium-coated polymeric matrix as a minimum volume-change and dendrite-free lithium metal anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yayuan; Lin, Dingchang; Liang, Zheng; Zhao, Jie; Yan, Kai; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10992</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal is the ideal anode for the next generation of high-energy-d. batteries.  Nevertheless, dendrite growth, side reactions and infinite relative vol. change have prevented it from practical applications.  Here, we demonstrate a promising metallic lithium anode design by infusing molten lithium into a polymeric matrix.  The electrospun polyimide employed is stable against highly reactive molten lithium and, via a conformal layer of zinc oxide coating to render the surface lithiophilic, molten lithium can be drawn into the matrix, affording a nano-porous lithium electrode.  Importantly, the polymeric backbone enables uniform lithium stripping/plating, which successfully confines lithium within the matrix, realizing min. vol. change and effective dendrite suppression.  The porous electrode reduces the effective c.d.; thus, flat voltage profiles and stable cycling of more than 100 cycles is achieved even at a high c.d. of 5 mA cm-2 in both carbonate and ether electrolyte.  The advantages of the porous, polymeric matrix provide important insights into the design principles of lithium metal anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpiFQKrkZVOgrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljTo2vFzgV48A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xks1ert7s%253D&amp;md5=665ac781f0a668a2caa866fa352b55d3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref52" class="rightTabRefNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit52" data-doi="10.1073/pnas.1518188113"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lin, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tao, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cui, Y.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Proc.
